NOT DEMOCRACY /- 

IF the present campaign on the constitutional amendments has 
shown anything it is the danger inherent in what is virtually 
a one-party government. What we have seen is the government, 
which is the Nationalist party, mai'shalling all its .strength, con- 
ferred upon it by the people, and moving heaven and earth to 
“crush” the Opposition, feeble and almost impotent as it is. 

Because of the weakness of the Oppo.sition one might think, 
that the government, in common fairness to the people, might 
actually have encouraged the Opposition, in order that the peo- 
ple everywhere mierht have a chance to hear both sides of the 
questions at issue. But no; apparently the government, with 
the po.ssible exception of President Quezon, had only one idea 
in mind, to “crush,” by fair means or foul, what little opposition 
there has been. That may be good politics, but, we doubt it. 
Certainly it is not democracy.

Cebu National Park 

Created By Proclamation 

President Manuel Lk Quezon to- 
day signed a proclaniatlon reserv. 
U g more than 15,000 hectares of 
public forest-cove red land in the 
municipalities of Balamban and 
Toledo, and the city of Cebu, Cebu, 
for park purposes. The park is to 
be known as the Central National 
Park, It shall remain under the 
administration of the bureau of 
forestry* 

The park is practically the only 
forested portion of the entire in- 
land of Cebu, the greater portion 
of the province being denuded of 
N^egotaiion. One of the main pur- 
poses in setting aside this ret;lon 
as a national park Is to prevent 
wanton destruction of trees, thus 
cllowlng remaining trees to grow 
unmolested bv the hands of men*

From Engaging in Rows 



pty edide iiy^- ycsterda> 1 

morninTlasuod an executive order, 
prohibiting offtclals from engaging 
in imneccssary debates among 
themselves or between themselves 
and private ciUzens in the press* 

The order follows: ^ 

In view of the frequency In 
which controversies touching upon 
p^.rticular phases of governmental 
aothity have been carried on 
trrough the public press by con- 
tending offtdals of the govern- 
ment, or between an official of the 
government on the one hand and 
a prutite individual on the other; 
and since this practice is unseem- 
ly, distasteful, and may oven, at 
times, be definitely harmful to the 
service; and because further, the 
publicizing by two governmental 
officials of conflicting opinion or 
real or fancied mutual grievances 
cannot fail to give the impression 
that the government Is devoid of 
order and organization, and that 
its members are lacking in that 
sense of roetraint and decorum so 
essential to tho effective discharge 
of public duty; 

NOW. THERFSPOTUS, I, MA- 
NUEL L. QUEZON, Phresldcnt of 
the Philippines, by virtue of tlie 
powers in mo vested by Jaw. do 
hereby direct tho following rulc« 
to be followed: 

(1) Controversies between offi- 
cials or employees of the govern- 
ment shall be submitted to the ro- 
spettive authorities empowered 
by law to take cognizance thereof, 
and the decisions of such author- 
ities shall be faithfully observed by 
tho contending parties. 

<2) If and when news Items or 
srtatementa contrary to facts are 
published In tho pros.7, or when 
criticisms are made, founded on 
false or incorrect Information. Die 
Nead of the Department or h!s 
duly authorized representative may 
issuo a statement giving the facts 



as U\oy actually are e.tid as sup- t 
ported by the official files, but in | 
no case, except with the express | 
authority from tho office of the 
President of the Philippines, shall 
such statement contain argument- 
Atlve matter or coutrovei'slal dis- 
cussion. 

This Order shall not preclude 
any official or employee of the 
government from furnishing spocl- 
llc Information on. or from clarify- 
ing doubtful points concerning any 
appropriate subject pertaining to 
tho function of tho particular offi- 
cial or employee, provided that 
such statements shall bo without 
rcfevvuce to previous oriticism of 
the government, or In answer to 
any of its critics. NclUier shall 
this Order preclude any dojKirt- 
mont or office of tho government 
from publishing from tlmo to time. 
AS it Is frequently Its duty to qo, 
articles la the daily press, in other 
periodicals or magazines, or in 
publications of tho government, 
which articles are intended to In- 
form the people regarding u;o poli- 
cies of and th« vs’ork being dons 
by the government on malices 
that may be of interest to the peo- 
ple in tho promotion of iheir well- 
being, such as topics regarding the 
efforts of the govemm. nt toward 
tho invprovcment of agriculture, 
development of proper commerce 
and trading practices, and other 
mnttora of similar general import. 

Any official or employee of the 
I government violating any of th? 

I ciDove rules dl.^cctly or Indirectly 
' abfiU bo subject to dliiclplinary ac- 
tion, Including removal from of- 
fice after due investigation and 
convection.

Sakdal Letters 
Held by Bureau 



Several registered letters ad- 
dressed to several Snkdal leaders 
in Manila, and articles for publi- 
cation in the Sakdal papers, be 
lieved to be subversive in nature, 
are now being held up \n the bu* 
rcau of customs for censorship, U 
was learned at the customs bureau 
yesterday. 

The letters and articles v/Hl e 
carefully scrutinized by the special 
cuatorns board of censors composed 
of Deputy Collector Jesus Obieta, 
Juan T. David, acting chief of the 
appraisers' and T. Capapas, chief 
of the law. 

The communications were mailed 
; from Tokyo. Japan, to Manila by 
; Benigno Htimos, supreme of 



the Sakdallsta'j, who Ih now. in 
self exile in Japan. They were 
addressed to R. Enrile. Ramos' 
secretary, who has just returned 
from Japan; to R. Haraos. 2326 
Juan Lwa, and to some Sakdal 
leaders in the city and provinces. 

In one of the articles written 
for the Sakdal paper, Ramos bit- 
terly attacks the present adminis- 
tration. He also urges his follow- 
ers in the Philippines not to lose 
faith in him and In their party. 

The letters were inter ^pted yes- 
erday in the malls at the Manila 
post office, by customs men de- 
tailed there. The missives will be

The existence of subversive elements 
in the Philippines with leanings toward 
mob violence to gain political or social 
ends is no myth or invention of politi- 
cal scare-mongers. There are people 
infected with radical ideas, for the 
most part originally imported, who evi- 
dently prefer chaos to law and order if 
it means gaining ponder and opportun- 
ity to promote their particular brand 
of social philosophy. Disclosures like 
those resulting from Saturday night’s 
i*aid in San Juan, where 11 alleged 
Sakdals were arrested and four bombs 
seized by Philippine Army agents, 
brings fresli to mind the danger of re- 
petition of the uprising of 1935. 

The danger, however, is perhaps 
exaggerated. The disclosures credited 
to the 11 men taken in the San Juan 
raid reveal little hint of either wide or 
potent organization among themselves 
or possible confreres. The oft-repeat- 
ed Sakdal scares arising from supposed 
leakage of plans to blow up Manila, or 
bomb Malacahan, or seize the banks, or 
wreck the water supply, are 95 per 
cent talk and 5 per cent planned action. 

When a plot is discovercfl. however, 
and men arrested who readily admit 
participation in plans of a subversivu 
nature, the question arises as to the 
pro])er punishment to he meted out to 
those convicted on criminal charges 
arising out of these activities, success'., 
ful or otherwise. The punishment is 
of course dictated bv law, but the gov- 
ernment needs a |X)licv in back of the 
law to determine whether extreme sev- 
erity of penalty .should be the rule, or 
whether justice should be tempered! 
with mercy in view of the fact that 
many of those involved are ignorant > 
people, misled by a few agitatoi’s. 

Such a policv is not easy to set. It 
demands a differentiation between 
those who ai‘e led and those who do the 
leading. Upon the latter falls a cloak 
of guilt far heavier and far more crim- 
son than upon those influenced by glib 
talk of oppression, real or imagined. 
Mhd glittering promises of power and 
influence to be obtained for the taking. 
For the leaders and agitators the rme 
of extreme severity is certainly applic- 
able when punishment for proved ot- ; 

:fense_is, considered.' They_sh^l^

improvement program. 



One of the evils eliminated with the passage of the- 
jnew election law is the habit of men electors to demand 
from the candidates payment for their delinquent personal 
[cedilla taxes in exchange for their votes. Heretofore these 
political parasites have intentionally neglected to pay their 
eedf/Ja taxes to the government for the years prior to elec- 
tions at the thought that when elections come there is a 
'Capitan Berino who would willingly stand for their obli- 
gation with the state. Of course, the poor candidate has no 
other alternative. His aim is to get the coveted position no 
matter how much it will cost him. With the approval of the 
new election law this unavoidable evil in the past is Comnlete- 

iy eliminated. And the credit gees to His Excellencv. Pjgsi- 
dent Manuel L..Quezou, who had conceived of this wise idea.

MINDANAO ROADS 





President Qaezoti k^i T\ie Fusion 

\ — : A Summing Up — ' 

— Reatitzt?^ Versus Theories hi Politics • 

By CARLOS P. ROMULO 

F or President Quezon’s courageous address read be- 
fore the national convention of the coalesced parties 
yesterday morning, the whole nation salutes him. His 
refusal to head 'the new Nationalist party and his bold 
j assertion of the independence of the President of the 
Philippines from partisan influence and dictation cons- 
titute a two-fold principle that may well set the norm 
of conduct of all his successors in that exalted office. 

It was indeed significant that on an occasion rife 
with partisan feeling, the President had the presence 
of mind not to be carried away by considerations of par- 
ty advantage. On the contrary, he endeavored to dis- 
entangle his ^position as President of the Philippines 
from his capacity as menvber of a political party. 

In refusing to head or to take any active part in 
the new political organization. President Quezon has 
demonstrated his good faith in desiring to consummate 
the fusion of the Pros and tlue Antis. We who have 
openly opposed the fusion are, therefore, ready to con- 
cede that his declaration of the independence of the 
presidency from the dictation of any political party has 
to some extent -mitigated our fears of the disastrous 
consequences of the political union.

OBJECTION TO APPOINTMENTS 
At the same time we consider it our duty to 
bring to the attention of the people 'wrtain practical 
aspects of the fusion that may not work out strictly in 
accordance with the theories enunciated. It was indi- 
■ cated, as a major defect of the Coalition, that the Pres- 
ident has encountered difficulties in ipaking appoint- 
ments to important positions because of the unavoid- 
able jealousies between the Antis and the Pros. 

The difficulty will be admitted by all who arc famil- | 

iar with the back-stage conflicts preceding the an- [ 

nouncement of every important appointment. That the . 
fusion vnll do much to ehminate this difficulty Is also 
conceded. It may well be asked, however, whether in 
the end we will not discover that we have paid too high 
a price for the privilege of making appointments free 
from objection or criticism. 

Furthermore, it is entirely possible that the par- 
tisan criticism of appointments will not be avoided eveh 
in a situation In which party lines have been. obliterated. 

An analogous situation obtaining In the United States 
today has shown that the bitterest obiectlon to an Im- 
portant appointment will as soon come from the rank.s 
of the party in power as from the party in opposition. 

Again, the system of checks and balances, which Is 
an indispensable element of democratic government, 
contemplates and justifies object lon.s, parti.san and i 
otherwise, to the actuations of the appointing power. 
Thus, while the Chief Executive is empowered to nom- 
inate candidates for positions in the government, the 
final authority is reserved to the National A.s.sembly to 
confirm or reject such, nominations. 

The Constitution specifically provides that the Com- 
mission on Appointments shall he composed of mem- 
bers elected thereto on the basis of proportional repre- 
sentation of the political parties ip. the National Assem- 
bly. The inference from this provision i.s clear: both 
as a matter of principle and as a matter of practical 
fact, the existence of objections of whatever nature to 
each and evo.ry appointment, made by the Chief Execu-

FUSION WILL NOT ABOLISH PARTISANSHIP 

The President expressed his satisfaction over the 
fact that the Coalition, has so fax succeeded in comply- 
ing "With the commitments made in its platform, 
will question the validity of the claim. But the Pres- 
ident also admitted that much yet remains to he done 
"during the remaining four years of his term. 

Tin's is to say that while the Coalition has made an 
excellent start towards the achievement of its pledges, 
It has yet a Jong way to go before the fruits of its labor 
can be conclusively secured to the people. 

President Quezon believes in good faith that the 
creation of the, new political party through fusion will 
much more effectively accomplish the commitments of 
the Coalition, ?i? 2 ve.^ again, we must beg t,o differ. 

The Coalition went before the people in the elec- 
tions of September 17, 1935, pledging itself to work in 
a non-partisan spirit on a common platform for the good 
of the nation. The Pros and Antis disavowed all par- 
tisan activity, pledging themselves to labor for the in- 
terests of all the people above and beyond every par- 
tisan consideration’. 

On this basis, the people whole-heartedly supported 
the Coalition. The Coalition’s offer of non-partisan ser- 
vice to the nation became, after the elections, the man- 
date of the people to the Coalition" itself . 

The act of fusion, we regret to say, has annulled the 
pon-paftisari spirit of the Coalition platform. Because 
the thought of party interest has been injected into the 
situation, it is natural, to expect that the residual por- 
, tion of the platform will henceforth be pursued in a 
heightened spirit of partisanship. 

For it is natural that the new political party will be 
Inspired not exclusively by the non-partisan character 
of the Coalition platform but by the de.sire, which is nor- 
mal. to protect its interests as a party and to secure 
for Itself every advantage it already enjoy.S. 

NOR WILL IT FACILITATE OPPOSITION 

The President repeated the now familiar argument 
that the fusion is intended to encourage and facilitate 
the growth of an opposition party. With the fusion of 
the Antis and the Pros into a single political unit, the 
boards of election inspectors will, in every future elec- 
tion, have at least one member who is neither a 
Pro nor an Anti but who will come from any opposition 
party that may be organized. 

That, however, thl ; circumstance will encourage the 
growth of an opposition group is plausible only in theory. 

In the various tovms and provinces outside Manila and 
the larger cities, the election.^ will still be fought, not 
on the ba.sls of national issues and not in accordance 
with national party alignments, but rather on the basis 
of personal antagonisms and petty political combina- 
tions. 

In the remoter districts v/here even the n-'w political 
party itself will largely be a convenient myth, the basic 
realities during election time will still be the per.sonal 
ambitions of the town bo.sses. Affiliation with any of 
the national political parties will be decided not prior 
but subse'quent to the elections, and will be determined 
by considerations of expediency rather than of princi- 
ple. 

Thus, unless ah opposition group is really given a 
fighting chance to establish itself in the field of poli- 
tical action,, the best wishes and the most fervent pray- 
ers in the world cannot avail to facilitate its develop- 
ment. ' . 

It is easy’ to say that no coercion has been used to 
drive anyone into the folds of the new party. Theoretic- 
ally everyone is -supposed to have acted in accordance 
with, his deepen ,A,s a. matter of practica^^

Thus, while many Antis as well 
honestly believed that the fusion was a dangerous thing 
for the nation, they have been compelled to smother 
their convictions rather than risk political martyrdom. 

The new Nationalist party is the most powerful po- 
litical organization ever established in the Philippines 
It is difficult to believe that anybody who is at all 
thoughtful of his political future would have dared come 
out against the fusion. That would be political suicide, 
and at this time there are not many who would vnll- 

fully risk such a fate. | 

Even with the support of ah American governor f 
general, the best thing that the Democrata party could 
offer was a nominal fiscalizatlon of the acts of the Na- 
cionalista-Consolidado party. It is fantastic to assume 
that any opppsitipn party could arise today to oppose 



the new mtlonaHst party literally starting from 
scratch and with no means or Instrument of power at 
its disposal. 

Years ago, the President boldly declared that hla 
loyalty to his party ends where his loyalty to his cg^n- 
try begins. Yesterday’s speech of the President reiter- 
ated the substanee of that famous slogan In tenn* and 
in a manner beyond cavil or dispute. 



OC/R LOP-SIDED POLITICAL DEVELOPMENT 



Nevertheless, the character of the fusion Is such ■ 
that, even in the face of President Quezon’s a-ssertion 
of independence from the dictation of his party or any 
party whatever, it constitutes a grave menace to our 
democz-atic institutions. It has created for the present 
and the immediate future at least, a Jop-sided political 
organization that may render Impossible the normal ■ 
processes bi criticism, opposition, and fiscalizatlon. 

Fortuvaateiy, \t is not too late to obviate, If only In 
part, the disastrous consequences ot the fusion. There 
is in Manila, at least, and doubtless also In the provin- 
ces, a growing political attitude that is cHher sutpJclous 
of or antagonistic to the fusion. If President Quezon and 
the Nationalist party are genuinely interested In per- 
mitting this as yet feeble spirit of opposition to grow, 
it should only be necessa^ to mlnlmizo the use of ex- 
treme political pressure by patronage, the pork barrel, 
and other means In order thdt tlie life o£ the qppoti- 
tion may not be snuffed out at the beginning. Al- 
though politics is as a rule generally governed by the 
principle^ of “All’s fair" and "No holds barred/’ ordinary 
sportsmanship would seem to require that the opposition, 
if an oppoRltiorv \r teahv desired, be given at iesMi^thc • 
barest opportunity to Survive. ‘*'4

Under American guidance, we have la this country CiUh- 
lished a form of government based upon our peculiar a4ftp~ 
tation of the experience of the leading democratic nations of 
the world. If anybody should doubt that we are fit for 
democracy or that democracy can succeed, let him doubt no 
longer. It is a lie to say that we are constitutionally unfit for 
democracy, that democracy i« alien to our emotional equip, 
ment, that in the uncertain ebullition of our tropical Malay 
temper < emocracy as a logical system, cannot thrive. Our 
brief experience has disproved all these allcgatiuna. Wc have 
found that there. is nothing Inherent in our intcllectdal or mo- 
ral nature which incapacitates ue for democracy, 

• m * 

There has not been, and there is not- now, any jU^fScatlon 
for the fear that democracy In the Philippines Is doomed. 
Menaced, perhaps, but only in the sense that democracy ia. 
menaced in many parts the world today, desperate to keep war 
out ,o£ the world, anxious to appease the lust for power of 
the dictators. But even as democracy is not doomed but 
calmly defiant against teacUon, so democracy In the ■Philip- 
pines ig not doomed but on the contraiy bound to become the 
permanent way of life for the Filipino people. 

Upon the first anniversary of his induction into of- 
fice, Pre.sident, Quezon declared that “•we are huUding 
here for all time.” May, we henceforth proceed to huild 
for all time to come upon the framework of democracy, 
slowly if r\ecctsa.Tv, hut BUrcly^anO -with the close . t a'.-

I particular phases of government 
activity, are “unseemly, distaste- 
ful, and may even, at times, be 
definitely harmful to the ser- 
vice.*' 

The presidential commandment 
provided that: 

“ (1) . Controversies between 
officials or employees of the 
government shall be submitted to 
the respective authorities em- 
powered by law to take cogniz- 
ance thereof, and the decisions of 
such authorities shall be faith- 
fully observed by the contending 
parties. 

'*(2). If and when news items 
or statements contrary to facts 
published in the press, or 
h^hen criticisms are* made, found- 
ed on false or incorrect informa- 
tion, the head of Abe department 
or his duly authorized TepteiscTk- 
tative may issue a statement 
giving the facts as they actually 
are and as supported by the of- 
ficial files, but in no case. 



except with the express author^ 
ity from the office of the Pres* 
ident of the Philippines, shall 
such statement contain argu- 
mentative matter or controversial 
discussion.^' 

Violation of the above set 
rules, adds the order, is punl^- 
able with disciplinary action. 

“As far as I am concerned/* 
said Municipal Board President 
Manuel de la Fuente, “President 
Manuel L. Quezon’s executive 
order. . . does not apply. 

“I am responsible only to the 
people and I have a right to 
speak my mind. Nobody is going 
to tell me to shut up when I have 
something important to tell the 
public."

Our Frozen-Salary System 

zi^l^aise Jot Having tnought 
Ijresid.ESK®**^ invpps ol the uovernnient. »>s P‘-n 

r oj'ihesmanempWMsMtnego 

to restoro Pvsvlons ^or^^ snoa-s hia inteml 
made seiviag untier Wm onrf a« doing 

t^a.foS^ve^“ne«peop^^ «P etneioai govern- 

must be adiiUtted that largo 

Vice employees flre woefully imdcrpaicl. Tlifi only correct 

remedy Is to Increase their pay. 

For fifteen years promotions in salary In the t »- 
ernment have been practically stagnant. Eacli succeea- 
ing Governor-General and finally' the Pteslder.l ordain- 
ed that salary increases may be given only In special 
cases. 

During fifteen years the rank and file of minor puo- 
Jlc servants have apparently been expected lo pass from 
youth to' middle age, or from middle age to old age, to 
get married, bring several children Into tly world, to 
feed, clothe, and educate them— all on the same Income 
they received fifteen years ago! 

The entire promotion practice of the Government as 
thus described Is preposterous. 

Is It any wonder that the past fifteen years have 
seen petty graft increasing by leaps and bounds? Does 
not this frozenrsaiary system largely explain the care- 
lessness, the neglect, the inattention to duty which have 
characterised so many branches of the public service 
during the past fifteen years? 

The government Is not broke. Far from it It la 
financially able to reward its employee* by pay commen> 
aurate wltlj their work and their length of service. We 
will not have a truly efficient public service until this 
Is done, and until a policy la adopted of making perio- 
dical promotions In pay to efficient employees a matter 
of course. 

411 praise be for President Quezon for thinking of 
the underdogs in our govenmient

ployer pays compensation to tbo

Americans Concerned 

That fact has beeHj and is innch 
more acutely today, a source of 
deepest concern to those ^vho task 
it is to watch over the economic 
and military destinies of the Unit 
ed States. 

Mean whiles the Phi iippijie Islands, 
which, according to United States 
experhs, are just as ideally suited 
for the cultivation of rubber as Ma- 
laya or Borneo, continues its ox- 
penments, with the cooperation of 
United Statas rubber interests, to 
develop a rubber growing industry. 

Latest reports received by the 
United States department of com- 
merce sho\y that the Philippines 
produced 752 tons of rubber in 
1936, In 1935 total crude rubber 
production amounted 654 tons; in 
1934, it was 580 tons. The increas- 
ing production is acceded by United 
>States experts to he an excellent 
indication, 

I Experts have repeatedly insisted 
! that both geographically and from 
< the standpoint of climate, there 
were portions of the Pliilippine Is- 
lands which could compete on equal 
terms with any rubber growing area 
in the world.

Goodyear To Cooperate 

Kecentiy it was^ announced tliat 
the Goodyear Rubber Company, one 
of the largest rubber processing 
1 corporations in tho world, would co- 
{ operate with Philippine government 
officials in even more extensive ex- 
peiuinenta^tion than has been carried 
on hertofore. 

Goodyear already owns an exper- 
imentaZ' rubber plantation in Min- 
danao where trees grown from the 
richest yielding seeds are being cul- 
tivated. This plantation is to bo 
expanded and others are to be es- 
tablished. 

Experts explained that mibbcr 
yield depends as much upou^he se- 
lection of seed and stock as^ upoi^ 
position and clilnate. The owners 
of the richest rubber grooving areas 
guard tTieir seeds as they would 
precious gems of. silver, recalling 
how Brazil lost a monopoly through 
the foreign seizure of the ITevca.

was, to be sure, less dynamic.

Opening Wedge 

The curtain fell after the

He was referring to the enemies 
of the HHC law, of which Pre.s-

credos went into war,

Invitatim for Coalition 
A group of Manila business 
leaders vjas ordered— now it 
can be told!-to issue the invi- 
tation for a coalition, smooth the 
m-inkles in the old party shib- 
boleth, and placate warring- 
groups. The scheme worked 
and the coalition was launched. 
Anti-coalitionists — Suniulong, 
again for one — predicted the 
eventual institution of a one- 
party system. But this was re- 
futed by coalitionists who, like 
Aquino, tried to disprove in 

lenghty discourses the theory 
that coalition means ending in- 
to a fusion. There will be no 
fusion, the Tarlac “bright boy” 
assured. 

With the election of Mr. Que- 
zon to the presidency and Mr. 
Osmeha to the vice presidency, 
the ends of the coalition were 
deemed s.erved. Coox>eration, 
harmony, a common goal. These 
prevailed. But wrinkles re- 
mained unsm'oothed, 

Atot Head of Both Wings 

First, President Quezon, 
while at the head of the govern- 
ment, was not virtually at the 
head of both wings of the par- 
ty in power. He did find this 
not only disagreeable but em- 
barrassing. He could not en- 
force party discipline, whip par- 
tymen into line. Or even if he 
could, he knew it was not pro- 
per. As President, he could 
prevail upon, say, the assembly- 
men. But he could not do it 
much in the same way that he 

uses the party whip. Tie found 
this particularly true in the 
National Assembly where, be- 
fore he could bring pro assem- 
blymen into line, he had to do 
the only proper thing; cour.se 
his recommendation through

Shortcomings

spect: the pro and anti divi- 
sions continued to be fenced 
grounds zealously guarded by

anti party lines have not dis- 
appeared in spite of the coali- 
tion. Reports given out during 
a recent reunion between Pres- 
ident Quezon and th“ ’''oder.s of 
both wings indicated that there 
are innumerable faithfuls in the

form of the coalition, maintain 
strict vigilance over their "lit- 
tle empires.”' Petty jealousies, 
and wranglings ba.sed on pa.st

filter into the administration o£ 
public affairs.

that disintegration wa.s inevi-

praisal of a judicial 

Whafthe whole aigiv 

ment boils down to is that 

Mr. Quezon, aroused by a de- 
cision which in his opinion denied elementary 
commented rather acidly on a 16th century frame ot tl e 
judicial mind. Exactly so President Franklin D. h-oose- 
velt commented on a decision of the Supreme Court of the 
JJnited States as forcing the nation hack into the “horse- 

and bu'ggy” age. . , , .4. , 

It is difficult to see precisely where any attack on De- 
mocracy and democratic institutions can be found iir an 
expression of views which are based on a common-sense 
conception of- justice. 

Has President Quezon attempted to impose liis will 
on any of the judges concerned? Obviously, not. Has 
he intimated that he would seek, the resignation of any 
judge whose decision displeased him, however unjust, in 
his estimation, that decision might be? There is no 
record of any such intimation. 

What, then, has Mr. Quezon done ? 

He has exercised the right of every citizen to speak 
his mind on a matter of vital importance to all citizens. 
Is there anything in the pf indpIeirof'Deniocracy that for- 
bids’ criticism of the courts? And if, unwittingly but in 
the best of faith,. Mr. Quezon uttered his criticism while 
the subject was pending decision, and thus broke a pre- 
cept of procedure, is it not nevertheless true that the 
minor infraction is eclipsed by his service in upholding a 
liiajor principle? Does a man commit a crime by break- 
ing the window of a bank- to stop the murder of the 
cashier ? ' 

So much has been s'akl about his encroachment' on 
■the principle of separation of powers in the government. 

Is it encroachment for an official of one department to 
criticize the acts of another department? If so, what 
becomes of the real spirit of Democracy, which in its e.s- 
sence is peedom to criticise any and every part of the 
government. When a judge criticizes the act of a legis-

people who are most iuclihed to wonder why, when one i 
public mail arises to protect them in securing those wages, 
so many other fine, wise, elducated people condemn him 
for if j 

Now, to the point of this condemnation. Is the Pres- | 
ident blamed because he took the side of a poor laborer | 
against a wealthy employer in the matter of a decision ! 
i. -which appeared to him to lack humanity? | 

The answer is. No. The President is criticized and j 
condemned because of the manner in which he attacked 
, the situation. 

,, Therefore It appears that it is not the basic issue of 
r human rights that is at stake in all ibis criticism, but the : 
m. procedure. The -implication is that the President could j 
i^have given his views on the matter in private, hut that it / 
was wholly, improper, practically subversive, of him to 
state those views publicly. It is maintained therefore, 
that a little more citciwispection, or, to use a blunter I 
term, a little mere hypocrisy, should have been practised .) 
*ito attain the end S’ought — ^which was a decent, social ' 
—orientation of the jndicml.mlnd in the final analysis. 

A>great deal has been made of the fact that the Pres- 5 
,V.Jdent’s, statement was improper because it might operate i 
,;.;to influence the minds of the justices who still have the 
-case in point under advisement. About the only answer 
^^that can be made to this stand is that it tacitly admits that 
’ 3 here can be no independent judiciary in the Philippines, 

‘ under any dycumstances. ‘ 

An honest and upright judge, an independent judge, 
holds- himself completely independent of anything but his 
iHipwn convictions; and no matter %vhat the President says, 
:^the country should have confidence that the Supreme 
Court will rule according to its convictions. If there is a 
j fear that anything, even a public statement by the Chief 
Executive, might influence the honorable justices, then 
there is no confidence in the court. We believe that the 
honorable justices are not so easily influenced. We be- 
lieve that whatever the President may .say about a giwn 
case* will not affect their decision in that case. i 

J.' The charge i.s made that the President, by his* .state- 
... iiient, has brought about a diminution of the public’s 
‘confidence in the courts. But it is hardly a secret that 
- the great mas.s of the people, the laborers, the tenants, 

’ the poor, have always regarded the courts with a degree 
01 suspicion precisely because it has been the impression, 
however ill-founded, that they functioned only to protect 
the rich. Lf the President, who cleman.4s respect for the 
ii.courts, talces the stand that he is for a judiciary that will 
^hold paramount human rights, the rights of the common 
^jWan to live decently, to be assured of his wages, to be ; 
Mpi otected in hi.v work; and if the Judiciary .sincerely j 
strive.s to adopt this modern outlook on its duties, how ^ 
can the conftd^nce of the people in the courts be otherwise = 
than strengthened ? If certain judges have the attitude ^ 
condemned by the President, and the public lacks con- ■ 
..hdence in the courts because of such judges, then, is not ; 

I that lack of confiidence at least in part justified? Is it i 
•not better to mistrust an unsatisfactory judge than to be- ! 

I lieye m him blindly because he wears the robe, and thus to ■ 

^ system that definitely belongs i 
with the dead past?, !

the justice-de fen ting' delny can bo olimihaicd. / 
if the people who must look to the courts / 
for the administering of justice are to respect j 
these courts they must be made to under- ' 
stand that the goal is not only ultimate 
justice but prompt decision in its favor. 
Tins applies in both civil and criminal 
branches. It applies to plaintiff and defend- 
ant, accuser and accused. It applies to all 
who do enter or may have occasion to eiiter 
the court room or to be concerned in the 
decisions iianded down there. ” 

It ' requires no extra legal procedure, 
neither extra modeni nor extra strict inter- 
pretation of the letter and the spirit of the ; 
laws, to make courts efficient and prompt 
jn administering justice. It requires only 
a conscientious attitude toward the oath of 
office and a combination of the willingness 
and the ability to work. hard. 

If President Quezon or any other inter- 
ested offiS""^t§Kds'^‘ contribute to the 
building of; a public respect for and con- 
fidence in the court system we should sug- 
gest that a fruitful^ field in which to w’ork 
would be that of court procedure or the 
branch of it which relates to causes of and 
excuses for delaying justice. We are cer- 
tain that an investigation conducted with 
the specific purpose of keying the court 
schedules to the very fastest tempo consist- 
ent with fairness and thoroughness should 
produce results. The w^ork done in this con- 
nection need not be controversial; it affords 
very little opportunity to be sensational ; but, 
it can be made fruitful and effective for 
social justice, abstract justice and the com- 
mon garden-variety of justice w-hich con- 
cerns the ordinary man in his daily routine.

married at LAST!— iCASADOS AL 



They're to be married at last! 

After dwelling together in more or less harmony for 
two years, the coalesced anti and pro factions of the Na- 
cionalista-Consolidado party will be.fttsed next week into 
the Nacionalista party. 

It's no secret that a good many of the antis didn’t 
view the permanent fusion of the coalesced parties with 
very hearty cheers. According to newspaper reports, at a 
caucus last week only five of 66 anti assemblymen were 
openly in favor of immediate fusion, while 28 of those 
present wanted to fuse after the provincial and municipal 
elections in December. But Preside^.Quezon has a per- 
suasive way about him, so the antis decided that discre- 
tion was the better part of valor. If any pros opposed 
the fusion, Vice-President Osnieha took care of them. 

Well, a lot of shot-gun marriages have turned out for 
the best. Certainly the fusion of the two parties will give 
a strong, capable majority all the power they need to 
govern the country efficiently and well. 

And what of the jilted suitor, the

The g’overnments of today 
are founded on the bromides 
of yesterday. Most dictators 
have risen to power on the an- 
cient truism that everybody 
wants something for nothing. 
Most of them are staying in 
power on the equally ancient 
truth that the pen is mightier 
than the sword. Side by side 
with their generals, the dicta- 
tors have put the spellbinders, 
the scribblers and the shouters, 
loud apostles of their gospels 
converting the political infidel. 

The Italians are kept happy yith 
odes on imperial Rome, the Russians 
munch black bread cheerfully for the 
sake of the world revolution, the Ger- 
mans listen to raucous Catilinarians on 
Jews and lost colonies and stay in 
line. Even in the U.S. the importance 
of ideas is recognized: a party press- 
agent writes the policy-making speeches 
of senators and governors, thousands 
of mimeogi’aphed relea.ses feed the 
daily press with the correct diet, the 
government has gone on the stage and 
screen. 

Sweetness and Light 

In the Philippines this vital mission 

of putting the right ideas jn people s 
heads is entrusted to the National in- 
formation board, and Mauro Mendez 
is its prophet-in-chief. By '''ytten 
word and word of mouth tins brisk 
bright apostle of Malacana.ng spreads 
a gospel of sweetness and light.

Nominal - heads of the board wore

versity, Mendez had served first as

new job, Editor Mendez brought the 
speed and resourcefulness of the city 
desk. 

He did not have the fast presses of 
a metropolitan daily: the government 
bureau of printing machines and men 
were used to the leisnrel}^ painstaking 
publication of official gazettes, .school 
circulars, bills and laws. But he made

of trained newsmen, who tapped out 
his stories in English, ^'written down” 
to the masses, for translation into the 
the different dialects by a corps of 
MalacaSang experts. 

Government Bible 

Besides the Balitang Panghayan

A 64-page booklet printed on glossy

latcd. into. - - 



Simple and substantial, the AkliH

prosperous peaee sucli as the Philip- 
pines enjoys, the riches of Mindanao,

civil sen’ice, the tenancy law, the usury

The Man in the Street Speaks 

.'T’-HE replies qjl the Man in the Street to the question: 
“What do you think of the President's criticisms of 
the Judiciary?” are surprising in~1Eat they are indi- 
cative of how poorly informed the average man Is ou 
public affairs. 

For three days previous, Manila newspapers had 
been carrying headlines on the subject. Then a Herald 
reporter approached a number of common people, a co- 
chero, a housewife, a taxi driver, a seamstress, etc., and 
to almost everyone of them the reporter had to explain 
the facts of the case giving rise to the attack on the 
judiciary before he could reply to the question. 

It Is significant that all those so questioned came 
out In favor of the President, or to be more correct, ex- 
pressed themselves as greatly outraged by the refusal 
of the defendant employer to pay the widowed mother 
of the deceased laborer adequate compensation for the 
death of her son. 

'Kiis Is plain common sense and was to be expected, 
especially as the Man in the Street cannot possibly have 
any other opinion on a case in which the right of one of 
his own class has, in his own eyes, been overthrown by 
the decision of a court. 

It Is equally significant, however, that the great ma- 
jority of those who read newspapers and who are more 
or less well Informed on public questions are critical of 
any move that would undermine the principle of the 
separation of powers and weaken the power of the 
■)urt3. 

At the same time, they feel, as strongly as the Pres- 
ident himself does, that the Interpretation of our laws 
should be so humanized that Justice can be secured to 
all, regardless of station. Were they given the op- 
portunity, however, to choose the means of human- 
izing the administration of Justice, they would undoubt- 
edly favor the amendment of existing laws In accord- 
arice with 20th century social ideas coupled with a 
statesmanlike invitation extended by the Chief Executive 
to the Judiciary to cooperate In the task of Improving 
the lot of the under-privileged classes. 

This is not to place the opinion of the better in- 
formed classes over those of the common people, but 
to show that while both groups agree on the necessity 
of establishing here a new conception of Justice in terms 
of the needs of the masses, the latter should not be ex- 
pected to relate the end thus indicated to such means as 
will safeguard our democratic institutions. 

Education of the common people Is a fine thing for 
a republic, and those who are wise have learned long 
ago not to underestimate the Intelligence of the people 
nor to overestimate their knowledge. For it llUle mat- 
ters how poorly informed the people may be. They have 
an unfailing sense of right and wrong. And any gov- 
ernment which expects to endure be built upon 

those principles which all, even the most illiterate and 
Ignorant, Instinctively understand and believe in.

never*hfrS?t National Information. Board 

^ wonderful break as the one it 

hiiict ^ result of the strongly pro-labor out- 

Durst of the President, 

For with one magnificent gesture. President Quezon 
has “sold” the government to the masses, and all that is 
; left for the NIB «jO do is to make the sale of record and 
to strengthen the mutual faitlr 4i<ecessary to make the 
compact effective and permanent. 

Thus, we are told that various radical groups, the 
left as well as the right wings of various labor organiza- 
tions, are planning a demonstration to endorse the stand 
taken by Presivnat Quezon and to present other pro- 
posals that will further advance the cause of the work- 
ing class. 

When you consider the entire controversy from a 
longer perspective, forgetting the larger issues involved, 
the thing that really stands out of the whole mess is the 
unerring Instinct for showmanship of the President, his 
splendid sense of timing such that every flourish, every 
gesture delivers upon the mind, of the spectator the lull 
impact of his sincere conviction. 

What results is bewildering, breath-taking drama— 
yet drama of such natural sequence and power that you 
are disarmed by the skill, of the actor, and remember 
only that the dramatic character of the incident arose 
from the very nature of the conflict itself rather than 
from any intent of the protagonist. 

Recall, for example, that only a week or so previous 
to the incident. President Quezon had called a number 
of Sakdals from San Juan who were arrested for illegal 
possession of dynamite and for an alleged plan to bomb 
Malacailan. At the meeting, he freely "gave it” to them, 
giving them fair warning that they would be punished ; 
to the limit in case of conviction. 

As precautionary measure, he announced that hence- 
forth he will refuse to pardon political offenders who 
must, because they have abused the executive clemency, 
serve their full term In prison. 

Then, a week later, he “gives it” to the judiciary— 
and presto! the 'tide of mass opinion turns upon itself. 
Labor groups, radical as well as conservative, vie with 
each other in paying homage to the President. Social- 
ists, Communists, and Sakdals join the chorus in praise | 

of “our” President. ' 

President Quezon has disarmed even his most viru- 
lent enemies. He can sleep in peace, his nights are resH 
ful. Think what load has been taken off the chest of ^ 
those whose duty it , is to protect the President and to , 

keep him in health! , ' , , j 4 .u^ 

In the political field, he has virtually l^ockedjjhc- 
props from the under the opposition, stolen their thun- 
der so to speak, and reduced them to the extremity oi^ 
criticizing and attacking him on general principle^ ; 
tirViioh nf rniirsp the masses cannot understand, or If they j 



do, care little for, , , ^ <.t,« , 

Thus, for a while at least, the field is clear of the. 

enemy, and those who would criticize anyhow-^n gen- 
eral principles — are compelled to adopt the technique oi ^ 



And here is where the NIB should come in 
handily — to “mop up,” as the war tacticians say, 
wide sector blasted of foes by the President, and secur-; 
ing through systematic follow-up the areas thus ga ^

MAS BIEN QUE MAL ; , 

(]0N la batahola creada por los comentarios del rresid«;nie Que- 
zon sobre el caso Cuevo-Barredo, pareee que se dej6 pasar 
por alto un hecho muy esencial. En Filipinas un magistrado 
sirve de juez y al mismo tiempo dc jurado; hace apreciaciones 
de hechos y de la ley. La cuestion principal en cl caso Cuevo- 
Barredo fue la de si Lozano perdio la vida en el cumplimiento 
de su deber, o si no estaba obligado a seguir las ordenes del 
capataz que le grit6 que se arrojase al Pasig para recobrar un 
madero. Un juez de Ja corte de primera instancia y cuatro de la 
corte de apelaciones, sostuvieron que el nbrern arriesgo su vida 
innecesariamente ; otro juez de la corte de apelaciones y el Pre- 
sidente de Filipinas, creyeron que el capataz fue el culpable, y 
el contratista debio reparar en lo posible el error de su capataz. 

En los Estados Unidos, cuando un jurado comete lo que 
al parecer es un grave error en sus apreciaciones de hechos, el 
juez no titubea en criticarlo. Pero en Filipinas, donde los jue- 
ces deciden tanto en cuanto a los hechos como en cuanto a la 
ley, el criticar lo que pareee un error debe quedarse para los 
ramos ejecutivo o legislativo del gobierno, para la prensa o 
para los ciudadanos de espiritu civico. 

eiaro esta qnc lo <iue. axitecede no quiere decir que deba 
haber criticas de las decisiones de casos en apelacidn, o co- 
mentarios perjudiciales sobre casos pendientes. Por medio del 
desacato los tribunales tienen amplios poderes para protegerse 
contra la critica maliciosa, injusta y promiscua de sus actos. 

Como el mismo Presidente Quezon Iq jxeonocio, es. de la- ^ 
mentar que el no supiese que SYaso Cuevo-Barredo estaba en : 
apelacion ante la corte suprema. Pero nadie que conozca a los 
miembros de dicho alto tribunal y su integridad e independen- 
cia, podra creer, ni por un momento, que aquellos decidan el 
caso en otra forma que no sea por la de sus propios meritos. 

* Anarte de todos los argumentos legalisticos que resulta- 
ron de la explosion del Presidente Quezon contra las decisiones 
ApI tirio de las del “siglo dieciseis,” creemos que solo queda por 

noEicioso o\ reBqVtatlo <U. vv<>Atu-irA mvioV,^

J^ODAY'S special issue of TAe Herald marks this news- 

oni/w!fif. ^i^estone. Anniversaries are useful if 
nly because they serve to mark a point where a man 

thAVoT^^^^®^ humble contemplation of 

s£ll fo future. This we 

shall do today but not in any spirit of boastfulness; we 

do it to remind the people that we have kept and will 
contuiue to keep faith with them. 

to om- ^ spontaneously written 
to oui publisher which appears elsewhere in this issue 
ly presents the history of the foundation of The 
Herald. Although his letter speaks eloquently for itself, 
we would like to cull the following paragraph as the text 
of our birthday message . • 

‘‘This story of how The Herald was ihunded will ex- 
plain Why there is abroad the erroneous Impression that 
I own The Herald, All I can say In this respect is that 
the PIO, 000.00 which I invested In it has been one of 
the saddest business experiences of my life. While I no 
longer own a single centavo in The Herald and have no- 
thing to say about its policies I can never forget the 
pecuniary sacrifice made the first owners of this paper 
In response to my appeal. And on this occasion my 
grateful thought is for those who in making it possible 
for the Filipinos to have at that time a newspaper in 
English to fight their battle lost every cent they put in 
this-enterprise.” 

A newspaper has to be owned by a person or a croim 

of persona, and we say in all sincerity that we wl^ 
that person or among such persons were the Presldeni 

paper was organized to lead 
in the PUlpinos’ fight for nationhood. As President Que- 
zon has been at the vanguard' of this fight, there has 
been a spontaneous alliance between him and this paper 
for this purpose. But the question of ownership has been 
i^atepi^. ■nxe task on hand was to convince America 
that the Filipinos had a right and were entitled to be 
free. Now that the task has been accomplished It is 
pertment that some explanation of the status of our 
consistent support of the Quezon leadership be now 



h\ 



4 . » grateful to the President for having himself 

taken the Initiative In this direction. Hisi explanation 
needs no further elaboration; it will sufhcd ‘!And it is 
more interesting still for me to find somethhes,” he says 
among other things, “that The Herald, in some of my 
important pronouncements as in the Barredo case, is 
less in agreement with me than its rival which has been 
looked upon by the public as invariably opposed to every- 
thing I stood for,” ^ 

May we say, therefore, that under the new Com- 
monwealth, The Herald hae. for sometime how traced an 
j independent policy. No longer must we shun unbiased 
criticism in the fear that it may jeopardize our cause in 
ji Washington. We have come upon a period of our na- 
tional life when we neeu self-criticism badly. So bellcv- 
^ Sng, The Herald has taken upon itself the duty of fear-, 

. lessly and independently commenting and elucidating on 
'public issues. The present incumbent of the Pres- 
'Idency of the Philippines, great as. he is, is only an in- 
cident in Philippine political history but The Herald, as 
an organ of public opinion, founded on deathless princu 
,Ple& is a Philippine institution. It wUl, therefore, re- 
gard the Presidency henceforth, in this wise, supporting 
the President "Without stint when it believes him to be ■ 
right, but criticizing him without fear when he Is wrong. 

The Filipino people fully supported this newspaper 
and the Pres!rtUc:3t. We believe that they realize as v/e 
do that a new policy ivas for sometime now been ip order.

BRAVE NEW GOSPEL — There is a common notion that 
men of action are of the type of the “strong silent men,” 
square-jawed, beetle-browed, ominous, averse to much talking. 
But as a matter of fact it is tlie great talkers who get things 
done and who manage somehow to nvake the world spin to 
the tempo of their utterance. 

It is the music makers and the phrase-makers who, as the 
poet so well puts it, are the world-shakers and world-movers 
forever it seems. It is the men who have the capacity to 
dream and an equal capacity to give their visions memorable 
utterance who succeed, far better than the strong silent men 
can ever hope to succeed, in shattering the world to bits 
and remoulding it nearer to their heart’s desire. 

As we swing over to the third year of the Commonwealth, 
no one man in the Philippines, it seems to me, is in a better 
position to undertake the shaking up and remoulding of our 
national life than President Quezon. To recall his utterances 
during the past few months and to list them down is to realize 
that they constitute a veritable new dispensation for the Fi- 
lipino people. It is as if a new prophet has arisen to create 
the body of a brave new gospel for diligent evangelists to set 
down and elaborate upon. 

Not to go farther back than three or four months ago, let 
ws Ifet down at least eight of the occasions when President 
Quezon came out openly, like another Man a thousand years 
ago, with fighting words and with words of wisdom, and also 
like Him, bringing a sword: 

1. The attack on the judiciary that has since jolted the 
magistrates of the land out of their centuries-old sleep and 
made them toe the line, not of the letter of the law, but of 
its spirit which gives life. 

2. The speech at the inauguration of the city of Iloilo, 
in which the master’s of the land were cnj'oincd to give a 
modest one-tenth of •what they have if they do not wish 
to lose all of it. 

3. The speech before the faculty of the University of the 
Philippines in which, like a prophet of old, he abominated the 
“frivolity of youth’ and set as an important goal of education 
the “Filipinization of the Filipinos.” 

4. The message to the National AsBcmbly in which he 
struck two separate notes: inviting free discu-ssion and even 
opposition to his proposal to shorten the tran.sition period, and 
rallying the people’s representatives to the assistance, through 
wise legislation, of million* of underprivileged Filipinos. 

5. The press statement in which he commented on the 
Chicago speech of President Roosevelt calling for a quai-antine 
of international anarchists, saying not merely that he approved 
ftc speech but that he would have liked to have said the same 
thing. 

6. His speech at the San Juan de Letran college in which 
he condemned an act which he believed to be an affront to 
him as President of the Philippines and pleaded for a national 
language. 

7. His message to the Assembly on the abolition of the . 
cedula tax, which marks the beginning of a general revision 
of our present system of taxation. 

8. His address on the second anniversary of the Common- 
* wealth in which he acknowledged, by offering the services of 

the Philippine Army to the United States, the strong bonds 
of sympathy which will forever link the destinies of American 
;and Philippine democracy.

The fact that the governors in office have 
had some advantage which they should not 
have had does not change the fundamentals 
of the case at all. Certainly it was a serious 
mistake to give them a term of office longer 
than that for which they were elected, but 
that mistake already has been made and rec- 
tification wdll not result from an added mis- 
take of stepping from the path of normal j 
function of the machinery of the democratic 



But we might as well recognize the practical 
fact that the best M'ay to build lasting con- 
fidence is to give the people what they have 
right to expect today and reason to Wpect 
to morrow, which is to say to establish pre- 
cedents .so thoroughly sound that they can 
and must be made permanent. 

If there is sufficient ground for taking 
away from a candidate or several candidates 
the advantage of office holding at the time 
of a campaign, then it should be definitely 
understood that such is a policy and that the 
present administration’s platform is to in- 
clude a re\’ision of the election laws to give 
that principle legal and pennanent standing. 
The principle is too vital to the cause of good 



government. TViere never was a place in i nment to be left to the uncertainties 

which the rule that two wrongs do not make i empoiaiy expec lency. such as the expe- 



a right is more thoroughly applicable than

confidence in orderly government by the 
normal functioning of its institutions.

Padres Y Madres 

Qiiezo.n Expidc Uaa Proclama 
A Este Efecto S endo El Dm El 
I .er Lunes De Dlciembre 



El inesideiite Hanuel L. Quezon, 
on una proclama expodicla el jue^ 
ves pasado ha decidido senalar cl 
primer lunes do diciembre dc cada 
ano como Din de lo.s Padrrv, quo 
mcluira a los padi-e^; y -j i>,.s q,,.,, 

I dres. 

Hasta ahora solo se ha obsarva- 
do anualmentc un dia dedicado a 
la.s madres, pero en adelante, cs- 
,te dia se dodicara por igual a los 
padres y a las madres. 

He aqui la proclama: 

"Por cuanto. mediante proclama 
No. 11. expedida el 20 de mavo, 
19-1, sc ha designado el primer lu- 
nes dc diciembre como Dia de las 
Madres: 

“For cuanto, se ban recibido peti- 
Oiones de varias fuentes para que 
se senale un dia aparte para la 
ooservancia anual del Dia do Pa- 
dres, basada en la creencia univer- 
sal dc quo si blcn la m. dre me- 
ce la cuna, el padre construye el 
hogar para la seguridad, comodi- 
dad y felicidad de la familia; 

"‘Por cuanto, no sc estima con- 
veniente fijar una fecha especial 
por separado para el dia de padres 
en vista de las fechas numerosas 
ya fijadas para fiestas; y 

Por cuanto seria solo convenien- 
te que se colebre conjuntamente el 
dia de madres y padres en lugar 
de hacerlo por separado; 

“Por tanto, yo, Manuel L. Que- 
zon, Presidente de Filipinas, en 
virtud de la autoridad que 'me con- 
fiere la ley, por la presente decla- 
re el primer lunes de diciembre de

When Pi'esident Quezon vetoed an ele- 
mentary School appropriation bill with aii 
election campaign just getting under way 
he did more to declare his administrative 
independence of political party than he could 
have done in most any other way. He dem- 
onstrated political coui’age and by hi.s act 
demanded soundness in allotting appropria- 
tions. 

Possibly there miglit be those who would 
attempt to minimize the significance of his 
action by pointing to the fact that the com- 
ing election does not involve the seats of 
the assemblymen whose action the President 
disapproves. That is true, but it also is 
true that the party considerations and affi- 
liations are substantially the same whether 
the balloting be for a provincial governor, 
or an assemblyman. I-he division of the 
proposed school appi'opidation as sought io 
the vetoed measure certainly suggests party 
considerations, and the President has pro- 
ceeded to ignore thi.s fact in holding the 
measure fundamentally defective on three 
counts, which he lists in liis veto message. 

The circumstances of this bill and the 
veto of it, after it had been materially 
changed from the form recommended by the 
President’s .special message to the assembly 
in extra se.ssion, bring to open discussion 
an essential point in the allotment of appro- 
priation funds. The point involves the ques- 
tion of whether appropriations should be 
made in lump sums and turned over to the ' 
executive department for distribution or, 
should be made in detail in such a way as; 
to limit more closely the discretionaiy money] 
powers of the executive department and at 
the same time to bring the legislative branch 
of the government, as the appropriating 
authority, in more intimate touch with the 
respective districts. 

\ President Qui^zon in a special message 
!o the assembly had recommended that the 
aione.y in this case be turned ever to the 
executive department in a lump sum “in 
view. of the lack of time then to investigate 
the needs of each and every municipality.” 

The lack-of-time explanation of that sugges- 
tion seems to imply recognition of the pro- 
priety of making appropriations specific 
under normal conditions, when time permits 
the necessary investigation of specific 
needs. It is generally considered that the 
appropriating power belonging to the legis- 
lative branch of the government carries with 
it the responsibility of surveying and deter- 
ininihg the specific needs in order to dis- 
tribute appropriations wisely.. Experience 
in this case would seem to prove that rnore 
thoroughness in planning appropriations 
^’ould have paid. Further, it suggests that 
her^fter more care, must be exercised. 

„ The veto message of President Quezon is 
n imptess.i.Ye. .s,uggestipn along th\s line.

'QUEZON Is FIRM 
ON NEW POLICY 

Not Interested In Any Man, 
Writes Ilongo Solons 

I . >j‘is determination 

take no part or interest in tho 
coming elections, President Qu^ 

* letter to all assembly- 
fin Iloiio today^ announced 

that the Naciohnlista party cannot 
have aai official candidate in that 
province unless one is so selected 
hy agreement between the '^anti^' 
and “pro" factions. 

The President s 'letter follows: 
‘‘This letter is intended for the 
provincial governor amd members 
of the National Assembly from 
Iloilo. 

Conformably w*ith the policy l> 
have already enunciated to. you in' 
person, I desire to reiterate the 
position X have taken in regard 
to the coming elections that I shall 
take no part nor interest i^ the 
elcctioii of any candidate for any 
provincial, city or municipal of- 
fice and that the Nacionalista Par- 
I ty cannot have an official candi- 
giving empIo 3 mient, so far as prac- 
ticable, to those in need of aid on 
public works projects and other 
work carried out by any branch 
or agency .of the Government. It 
shall further seek to enlist the oo- 
operation of private employers in 
the distribution of employment in 
such a manner as to provide the 
maximum amount of relief from 
iinemploj’tnent. 

“The National Unemployment 
Board is hereby authorized to es- 
tablish branches or agencies in 
the provinces, chartered cities, and 
municipalities with a view to car- 
rying out within their respective 
jurisdiction the purposes for which 
the said Board is created. 

' ''All officers and employees of 
^ the national, provincial, and mu- 
I nicipal governments are hereby » 
enjoined to cooperate in every 
possible with the National Unem- 
ployment Board, and to faithfully 
perform such duties as may be 
assigned to them by the said Board 
with the approval of the corres- 
ponding department head. 

“Executiye Order No. 516, senes 
cf 1934, and all other orders which 
are inconsistent herewith are here- 
by revoked. 

“Done at the City of Manila, 
this 8th day of October, in the 
year of Our liord, nineteen hun- 
dred and thirty-seven, and of the 
Commonwealth of the Philippines, 
the second.’*

nucleus of the organization of the 
G-Man division of the department 
of finance by starting the selec-

cants with which to begin the loc- 
al unit. 

Captain Dugan was contracted 
b.v President Quezon in New York 
to orga7rt5re«^4hii«JlacaL*investigation 
division in cooperation with Gucr- 
nu’o. who was formerly connected 

with the federal bureau of inves-: 
ligation, department of justice. 

I A majority of the applicants are ^ 
I college graduates, including law- ' 
j yens, while a number have been 
I either in the service of the Philip- 
I pine Constabulary or the Manila 
police force. The first fifty appli- 
cants to be cho.sen will be sub- 
jected to physical and mental ex- 
aminations. 

To insure an intelligent and effi- 
cient crops of special agents of the 
G-Men unit a high and rigid stand- 
ard v/as adapted by the department^ 
of justice. The elibility rules call 
for an extensive experience and ! 
training in law enforcement and ai 
\oUcgc or university education.

Que Se Realicen 



El programa de mejoras anunciaclo ayer por el Presi- 
dente Que;5anH4ja-ra la' ciudad de Manila, co'ii motive de la 
colocacion de la primera piedra del niievo City Hall, pro-

cias.

ras anunciadas se realicen, una nueva era de bienestar y 
felicidad aguarda a esta comunidad que siempre ha coope- 
rado con sus autoridades y que es merecedora de esa aten- 
don y de esas consideraciones que ya se vislumbran. 

Las mejoras a que nos referimos consisten en lo si- 
guiente: Construccion de dos nuevos puentes, uno que sus- 
tituya al Colgante, y otro que permita el acceso de los ca- 
ches del ferrocarril a los pantalanes; Construccion de uu 
gran paroue de reeveo al estiJo de los de Eui’opa y Japon ,

P100,000 instead of the P51,000 
alloted them under the vetoed bill.

Habi'a quienes crean que el veto presi-

probada. Asi es eu, efecto, perc tambien es

gpbernadores provinciales. La division de 
consignaciones, tal como se proponia en el 
pi’oyecto que ha sido desaprobado, implica 
ciertamente, que mediaban consideraciones 
de partido, y el Prosidente ha prescindido de. 
ello declarando nue el proyecto era funda- 
mentalmente defectuoso por tres conceptos, 
que el seiiala en su mensaje.

Assembly Will Seek Sum 
Double That Vetoed 
By Quezon 

MANY BEWILDERED 



President's Action 
Beyond Understanding 
Of Some Members 



Assemblymen lost P5, 000, 000 in 
public works by President Q^^zon^s 
veto of tlie special* works act but 
they figure on gaining PIO, 000,000 
when they meet in regular session, 
i according to reports circulating in 
! legislative circles last night. That 
is, unless the President vetoes a 
P10,000,000 approx>riation bill as he 
did the P5, 000, 000 one because, as 
the' chief executive explained, the 
aseinblymen did not know how to 
add. 

Eeports from reliable quarters in 
the legislature yesterday indicated 
that in addition to the Po, 000, 000 
for school buildings voted in the 
vetoed worsk act, the assembly will 
propose a similar sum for the reg- 
ular public works appropriation for 
next year at the regular sessions of 
the assembly to open on October 
16. 

Under this proposal, the members 
of the assembly will get each PlOO,- 
000** instead of the Pol, 000 alloted 
them under the vetoed bill. 

Assembly Bewildered 

The ^rational Assembly, however, 
is likely to be called to consider 
works appropriations much larger 
♦ than th-e regular public vrorks out- 
^ lay set aside every year because 
: Presideiit Qubzon -proposes to 
mit a program of road construction 
covering a period of four years and 
; involving f70,000,000. He announc- 
ed this to the press last week and 
it is understood he will recommend 
tliis to the assembly in his message 
when that body op^s. 

The veto by the President of the 
public works bill bewildered mem- 
; bers of the National Assembly, who 
did not know whether they 
get angry or laugh off the chief 

executive’s action. 

Two outstanding hiembers of tn 
assembly yesterdny asked questions 
instead of answering those put to 

^^'«What shall we say?” said one 
of them, obviously helpless but 
showing nevertheless hurt pride. 

Held As Bait, Claim 

“Many would be drawn to the 
conclusion that the President held 
out the P5, 000,000 works bill as 
bait to facilitate the 
the pensions restoration bill,

Abuses in the government will be harped upon by Ibe 
■Popular Pront as the opposition party’s main electoral issue 
in tlio December elections I'or provincial and municipal posts. 

—TlllBUSF. 

Only we remember that the late lamented Democrata 
party went down to an inglorious end harping on the same 
stuff. The Democrata big guns finally got some sense 
and when the opportunity came, joined ^Ir. Que^n and 
Mr. Osnefia. 

If the Popular Front people are laboring under the 
illusion that harping on the abuses in the government 
will fetch votes for them, then they will deserve the 
thorough drubbing they will get in the December polling. 
Unless they have an ace or two up their sleeves, enough 
two-peso bills, for instance, with which to pay for cedulas 
and feed the electors at rallies and on election day, the 
Popular Frontists may as well lay low during the next 
three or four elections and devote all their time to im- 
proving the health of the party treasury. 

For the irony of it is that these very masses w’ho 
are the victims of government abuses liave a very re- 
markably short memory. Confronted with a swell feed 
and a free cedula, they forget the abuses, and march 
peacefully, even enthusiastically, to the polling booths to 
bless the existing tyranny. 

There is the very obvious fact that the Popular 
Frontists on the strength of their public record are not 
the kind to make the voting public believe that here at 
last are political prophets who will lead it to democratic 
Utopia. In fact they present the terrifying picture of 
a hungry pack, and the average elector with Kis notorious 
stupidity is not sufficiently stupid not to recognize the 
wolves sniffing at the door, which he knows are not at 
all different from the wolves already in the pantry, ex- 
cept that the ones at the door are considerably hungrier. 

M. S. M.

formes facilitados por los fiincio- 
narios de la Sj.nidacl ayer. Por 
encima de los motivos sentimen- 
tales, de permitir que los enfer- 
mos esten segregados en un lugar 
proximo al pueblo donde tiene su 
hogar, el plan recluir a los en- 
fermos '6n leproserias sisLematica- 
mente situadas, parece correr el 
peligro de sufrir una revision. 

Las condiciones que se requie- 
ren para conseguir. la liberaliza- 
cion de la politica de segregacion 
de los enfermos d^e lepra, son: (1) 
Seguridad de estrlC^ta supervision 
y control sobre los leprosos y las 
leprosarias, ,con especial enfasis 
referente a la ccnducta^ de los en- 
fermos con los parientes que van 
a visitarlos, y (2), presupuesto 
adecuado para la construccion de 
leprosarias, o el cnsanche de las

lepra, para ser convertidos conve- 
inentemcnte en leprosarias, y asig- 
nacion de sueldos para los lepro- 
logos y medicos que tendran la 
supervision de las leprosarias. 

En la discusion de las varias po- 
sibilidades de infeccidn, uno do 
los funcionarios dc la sanfdacT ex-

dad do los .enfermos a los pueblos

c'esidad. En tales visitas, se ex- 
plico, los parientes se exponten 
personalmente a un contagio. Es- 
pecial atencion se ha dedicado cn 
la cuestion de los nihos de uno 
hasta cuatro ahos de edad, los cua- 
Ics por su natiiraleza sen altamen- 
te propensos - a la infaccion. 
"‘Seria tragico," ebservo el funcio- 
nario, “que en nuestro esfuerzo 
de mitigar el, mal moral del enfer- 
mo y de sus parientes, les coloque- 
mos en una miser ia mayor, y au- 
mentemos la responsabilidad del 
gobierno.” 

Una caracteristica peculiar del 
leproso, que tambien se puede en- 
•contra en los enfermos tde (tu- 
berculosis, se sito como argumen- 
. to en contra del proyecto de co- 
1 locar las leproserias cerca de los 
comunidades establecidas. El le- 
proso, por la misma naturaleza 
del mal que esta padeciepdo, pa- 
dece de desequilibrio sexual y de- 
be de ser sometido a rigidas res- 
tricciones. 

Los expertos de la oficina de 
Sanidad sostienen el punto de vis-

fermos no rnuy avanzados que

The first task fa-ced by the coun-

MI QUERIDO PAPA : 

No todos los aiios hallo oportunidad de salir 
de este mi riiicon, asi es que cuando mi jefe me 
dijo hace algunas semanas de que podia irme de 
aqui — a cualquiera parte y durante todo el tiem- 
po que yo quisiera — casi me volvi loco de con- 
tento. No recuerdo ahora por que de todos los 
lugares escogi Surigao, para alia olvidar por al- 
gunos instantes mis pesares, Naturalmente que 
U 21 viaj 0 a Hongkong o a Shanghai estaba fuera 
de orden — eso es solo para los ricaehones que no 
tienen nada que hacei- y que tienen dinero de 
sobra para tirar. Ademas, aun duando pudiese 
gastarme el viaje^ no hubiera corrido el riesgo 
de ser alcanzado por proyectiles perdidos como 
casi sucedio con mi buen amigo, el Sr. Oppus. 
Pero, 6 por que no Cebu, Iloilo, Zamboanga, o Da- 
vao? queras saber. 

Como ya he dicho, fi’ancamente no se por 



Yulo y del Se-'-etario Rodriguez. Para probar tal 
vez su asercion do que era iiifluyente on los cir- 
culos gubernamentales, indico que cierto co- 
merciante de Surigao ha sido investigado por las 
oficinas de forestal y de rentas internas a su ins- 
tigacion, y para hac'erme creei* su alegada in- 
fluencia con los “peces gordos”, hasta me dijo 
que aunque el informe de la investigacion no 
habia sido aun sometido a las autoridades corres- 
pondientes, podia ver el esbozo del mismo si me 
fomaba la molestia de acompaiiarle a la oficina 
forestal. Lo que era otra forma de decir que tenia 
a los miembros del comite en las cuencas de sus 
manos. 

Mi curiosidad se acentuo mas, y continue 
inquiriendo de la gente quien era verdaderamen- 
te este( hombre, y que era lo que la comunidad 
pensaba de el. Papa, parece ser la impresion ge- 
neral en la cabecera de la provincia cine este 
Consing e.s vei'daderamente tan influyente que 
no hay casi nada que no pueda obtener del go- 



que, pero lo en cierto es que una vez que estaba particularmente, segun el parecer de mu 



ya a bordo de un barco con rumbo a un destino 
indefinido, Surigao vino subitamente a mi ma- 
gin. Asi es que una tarde me vi caminando por 
las playas de ese pueblo que antes estaba muy 
muerto, pero que ahora rebosa de una afluencia 
continua de humanidad que va alia con el mismo 
proposito — el hallar la mina de oro. No te voy a 
decir aqui mucho del pueblo o de su gente, aun- 
que dire que no tardara muCho la provincia, a\ 
paso que va, en ponerse a la altura de sus herma- 
nas provincias mas prosperas. Prefiero contarte 
sobre mi experiencia interesante con cierto per- 
sonaje con quien tropece alle, quien, si hemos de 
creer lo que dice y pretende ser, parece tan pode- 
roso como el mismo Presidente Quezon. 

•Trate con este hombre, su nombre es RAPHA- 
EL CONSING, desde el momento en que pise.Su- 
ngao. Un amigo y combarcano mio me habia 
estado diciendo que este hombre,— de paso te 
dire que posee la planta de la Surigao Electric 
i Light y esta asociado a varios intereses japo- 
neses Pn el comercio madarero — 'es tan influyen- 
te entrei los funcionarios de Manila que puede 
conseguir del gobierno lo que 1e place. Tanto in- 
teres desperto en mi, comparable solo al interes 
que ssnti cuando por primera vez oi sobre el 
primero y unico emperador que Pilipinas hamas 
ha tenido— Flor Intrencherado— que hice todos 
los esfuerzos posibles por conocerle y con

glamentos "que se dictan si no en la seleccion y ealidad del 
personal. Tambien la e,jcperiencia larga con <3ue euentan

POSTPONED 

On accotint of an indiapOvSMlo*i 
of Mrs. Manuel L. Quezon, the, 
President has postponed his trip 
to Iloilo until tomorrow morning. 

The President arrived in a spe- 
cial train from the Bicol provin- 
ces yesterday, moining, and pro-; 
ceeded immediately to his farm 
in Arayat, Pampanga, to see; 
Mrs . Quezon, 

The President is going to Iloilc 
in response to the invitation oi 
Mr. and Mrs. Enrico Pirovano tej 
act as godfather to their baby. . 

Incidentally, the President will 
confer with provincial and mun- 
icif/al officials during his stay in 
Iloilo, and possibly with officials 
of Negros Occidental. 

Political circles repoiled yes- 
terday that the conflict over the 
candidacy for governor in Negror 
between the Alunan-Montelibano- 
Hernaez faction and ' the Yulo- 
Araneta-Lizares gfroup mav he 
definitely settled when 
atives of the two factions confer 
with president Quezon this mor- 
ning. 

Secretary Ramon Torres ir. 
mentioned as the most likely 
compromise candidate. If no set- 
tlement is arrived at, the Na- 
cionalista part in Negros will be 
in.structed to hold 0 , convention 
to select the official candidate.

la at 7 a. m. yesterday from the 
Bicol region in a special tmln. 
He left the train at the Paco ola- 
lion.

... 6’pent abroad, 

j. igiuiicantly ho eamo from Sliang-

AMtiiess Japanese agression and 
t nental diseord at its heiglit. AVith 
these stormy events in mind, he re- 
iterated his belief* that the Piiilin- 
idiies should listen to new proposals 
for the future and abandon thouglit 
of independence. 

The Philippine President is en- 
dowed, with tlio wdiness of the 
East. For more than a quarter cen- 
tury his dream has been one of Fi- 
li])ino idepcndence. AVhon he was 
Philijqiine Commissioner in the

ican rule over tlio Islands. ^ In lat-

to Washington to preach his cause. 
He talked witli Senators and Ee- 
presentatives', with newspapermen 
and business leaders. lie was the 
untiring cJiampion of indo2)en den cc. 

Six weeks ago he reversed his po- 
sition, and expressed doubt as to

that Ids country is not ready, is 
not strong epough for self rule. 
The effoj ts of Japanese busincssMn- 
terests to dominate the Filipino su- 
gar, cotton and, iron markets have 
02)t*ncd his eyes. 

President Quezon is to be com- 
mended for his courage in tm*ni ng 
his back on a cause for which he. 
fought so long and so vigorously. 
It remains to be seen, however, 
whether Ids change of heart will 
have any effect upon the American 
Congress*, which already has fixed 
the date when tlie United States 
will withdraw from the Islands. 

It is doubtful if <CongrcSvS will 
consider the Philippine question. 
The Filipinos demanded freedom, 
and it was finally voted to them 
by Congress. It was generally ad- 
mitted at tliat time that the Fili- 
pinos were far bettor off tis wards 
of the United States than they 
would bo under an independent 
home government. Now they are

tiie case. Let us hope that the de-

Front Criticises Quezon 



President Manuel L. Quezon was 
again taken to task in a statement 
issued last night by the Popular 
Front, charging him with incon- 
sistency with his avowed princi- 
ples of social 3ustice. The Presi- 
dent, in spite of his denouncements 
of the “mediaval mentality” of the 
judges, has not taken steps towards 
the revision of the court system, 
the Front statement claims. 

The statement follows: 

President Quezon talks of social 
justice in and out of season but he 
does very little to carrv into effect 
his professed good intentions. Take 
the Cuevcf-BaiTedo case.. After de- 
n oiinci ng^lKe'^Rl^i^al mentality of 
the judges who decided that case, 
and others "of their ilk, did the 
President announce or advocate 
any revision of the court system to 
prevent the perpetration of further 
mischief by such judges? Did he 
at least give official orders to his 
Secretary of Justice in line with 
his pronouncements with regard 
to said case? Bourbon judges con- 
tinue exhibiting their class preju- 
dices, and the workers suffc' ing 
thereby. The tory opponents of , 
President Quezon would indeed be 
right to accuse him of encroaching j 
^pon the independence of the judi- 
ciary, if he tries to curb judicial 
wrongdoing by personal denouncia- 



tion alone and not through legisla- 
tive means. 

Again, as the Spanish say: ''una 
cosa es predicar y otra es dar tri- 
go.*' It is not enough, to preach 
social justice in the style of the 
once famous, now almost forgotten 
Father Coughlin, the fascist-mind- 
ed priest of Detroit. Definite steps 
must be taken to ameliorate the 
living conditions of the working 
masses, especially the tenants. The 
toilers of the soil must be given 
land by the gradual nationalization 
of agricultural lands. Employment 
must be provided to the workers 
and relief given to those who can- 
not be employed. 

While the Roosevelt New Deal 
government spends billions of dol- 
lars to supply work and relief to 
the workers, the Quezon admin- 
istration is only concerned to save 
the last centavo in order to balance 
the budget. Balanced budgets are 
all right but if they are accom- 
plished at the expense of much 
needed social improvements, the 
balancing of the budget benefits 
o^ily' the financial interests.
