PP3Z 

a<i NOT A DEEAM— Today 1 want to Indulge a feeling ' 
a Trmo- something Of incalculable value after 

of unuttPrS/f waiting. i want to luxuriate in a sense 
pLL happiness that is as com- 

Lss tharnrppL as the happi- 

P edes the waking from a beautiful dream. 

President Quezon has spoken with vigor, authority 
and, I am-e&gtaim. Tma u w i t k4^ also. It was 

natural, at first, to suspect him of being a cosmic paint- 
. er placing upon the horizon broad washes of color to 
make the eyes of the people glo^’- with the hope that 

2rth beneS^ to_ break at last upon the parched 

Nor was that the first time the wox’d cosmic has 
been applied to him. He earned- the epithet long ago 
after weathering several terrific political storms that 
would have crushed a man of less vision, courage, and 
passion than he. But never before did he appear qui^e 
as grand as he did yesterday when he stood up for the 
freedom of his country and for the liberation of great 
masses of his people from economic servitude. 

I like to think of him who spoke with such clear and 
vigorous accents yesterday as Manue} L. Qxxezon, the 
man, who came from the loins of the common people 
and has not, through many years of comfort for him- 
self and his own, forgotten his first roots or denied his 
Inheritance. 

Yesterday this same man came down from the seat 
of power to hold the poor, the sick, and the ignorant up 
to the gaze of their betters and- masters, and to say: 

These have too long been denied tUelr due. i will 
not tolerate this denial one moment longer. For all of 
them let us henceforth provide adequate means for sub- 
sistence and well-being. We must bring them forth 
from the cave of darkness where they have dwelt. Into 
the world of light. 

I place the precious words upon my palm and finger 
them closely one by one. I want to be sure ''they are 
genuine, that their sparkle is not that of cheap colored 
glass. And I am persuaded that this is a priceless treas- 
ure I have in my hands, and that the sparkle I see Is the 
fire of sincerity breaking through. 

Yet more priceless even than these is the triple 
freedom of which they speak: the independence of the 
Philippines, the civil liberties of the Individual citizen, 
and the economic freedom of the masses. 

I pinch myself still hoping fervently this is not a 
dream, but that a man Prometheus-llke has indeed come 
to bring the boon of true freedom to all our ^ople. ^

AMMUNITION TO 
OPPOSITION 



THE 



Members of the National Assembly 
will be inviting criticism and throw- 
ing themselves and theii* party open to 
attacks from their political foes if the 
assembly passes a few appropriation 
measures and then recesses for assem-' 
blymen to go home to campaign for 
their friends in the provincial-munic- 
ipal elections. ^ _ ; 

The interpretation naturally given i 
such a course of action would rim inj 
precisely the opposite direction to the' 
theory advanced recently by President! 
Quezon when he decided to remove 
fi’om office temporarily the provincial 
governors who are campaigning for 
reelection. 

If the law-makers endorse the social 
justice program presented by President- 
Quezon sufficiently strong to stand 
I’eady to translate theory into construc- 
tive legislation they have a heavy task 
ahead of them, so heavy that they hard- 
ly can afford the time to interrupt the 
session to allow time for campaigning 
for provincial and municipal candidates- 
in their revSpective districts. 

The best possible campaigning they 
can do is that of performing well the 
task which is theirs in the name of pub- 
lic welfare, including social justice. 
The best campaign ammunition that 
possibly could be given the opposition 
is that which goes with the opportunity 
to charge that members of the National 
Assembly place the interests of their 
candidate friends in the provincial and 
municipal races above the interests of; 
the high public trust of their offices] 
and the serious public task of prepar-j 
ing and enacting legislation for the peo-j 
pie of the whole Philippines. If the; 
assembly takes a campaigners’ recess; 
after, voting through a bunch of appro-; 
priation measures, that course of action; 
will invite such charges, whether or not; 
they be true for any or all members of 
the assembly. 

This very definitely is a case in 
which the law-makers should not only 
avoid evil but the appearance of evil., 
They should hold their offices, theiP 
official acts and the legislation which 
they approve above possible suspicion,' 
should see that there be no room for; 
any opposition campaigner to charge j 
them with using appropriations to l)uy 
Votes and taking a legislative recess to 
allow time for cashing in on the pur- 
chases.

QUEZON’S ‘NO’ EMPHASIZED 

President Quezon’s refusal to consider 
proposals ■ for a second term in office was 
far more emphatic and coiiclusive than “I 
don’t choose to run.” He says unqualifiedly 
and clearly that he will not run and will 
' not permit himself to be considered as avail- 
able. He makes it final — as he properly 
should do. 

i It Avould be a mistake on the part of 
President Quezon to take any stand other 
than that which he is taking-. It Avould be 
a mistake on the pai-t of his friends and 
the Philippine government to attempt to 
modify the constitution to make possible 
his reelection. This comment is in no wise 
ia i-eflection on the Quezon administration, 
ilq fact it is made on the basis of the fun- 
damental principles involved and with no 
reference whatsoever to the coaalifications of 

j the man. , . ^ 

] It is perfectly clear that President Que- 
zon was speaJ^ing• on the basis b7“pfihciples 
\t^n he stated the vital truth that any dem- 
j ocratic government which grets itself into a 
■ one-man frame of mind is in a dangerous 
condition. He was speaking on the basis 
of principle again when he called attention 
to the embarrassment \vhich would be caused 
if the constitution should have to be changed 
SO very soon and for reasons relating to 
one man^T-no matter who that man may 
be and how good his administration may be. 

President Quezon’s emphatic refusal in- 
I dicates very cleaidy that his friends, nnd 
' admirers who attempt to stampede him and 
force the issue are causing him decided 
embarrassment. He apparently considers) 
that they also are causing embarrassment ; 
^ to the government. ) 

i There should never have been any occa- 
! sion for him to feel forced to come out so 
very strongly, but under the circumstances 
he has done the proper thing. Any other 
course on his part might have been mis- 
understood.

Of especial importance is his estimate of the true sig- ;

It is the sworn duty of the Chief Executive to act nl- ; 
ways for the protection of the general welfare. _ It is in- 
conceivable that Mr. Quezon or any Chief Executive should 
discount the importanae to the general welfare of a pros- 
perous' foreign conimerce. It is unthinkable that be should 
even consider any step, any measure which Avould imperil 
interests which contribute as much to the general welfare 
as' do the foreign businessmen and investors in the Philip- 
pines. Therefore, when Mr. Quezon tells the Assembly that 
the ten-year transition period ivas established for the bene- 
fit of the Filipino people, and not for that of foreign in- 
vestors who do not have confidence in the Filipino nation, 
he is in effect inviting foreign business in the Islands to 
join in what he .sincerely believes to be a progiv.m de- 
signed for the best interests of the Filipinos, and thus also 
of the foreign businessmen Avho do business with the Fili- 



^ Far from being, therefore, a challenge or a defiance to 
foreign investors to “take it or leave it,” Mr. Quezon s ex- 
pressions are I'eally an invitation to foreign investors to re- 
dedicate their faith in the country in which they have pro- 
spered, and to staj'^ and share both in the task of building 
a stable economy in the Philippines, and in the benefits 
Avhich will accrue to ail from such an economy. « 

Comment on much of the President’s message insofar 
as it deals Avith fundamentals must be left for another time. 
It is sufficient for the present to open for discus.sion one ot 
: his most important declarations. A careful examination 
, of that declaration should leave no doubt as to the intent 
•behind the Avords, that foreign Im.siness in the Philippines 
■may be asstired of all the pi’otection which an enlightened • 
jdeiiiocratic government can provide for it.

COMMENTS 



Oe^ 










BICAMERAL LEGISLATURE? 



T he President announced that he is in favor of 
reviving the’ bicameral legislature. This in- 
volves an amendment to the Constitution, of 
course. 



jAside from the fact that the unica'meral As- 
sembly we have at present has not functioned 
long enousrh to show inherent defects, or at iea«:r 
enough inherent defects to wai’rant its re- 
placement by the old bicameral system of 
mahing, there are other considerations which 
would make postponement of the proposed 
change prudent,. 



One of these is the fact that the chief execu- 
tive can. when the circumstances are favorable, 
control a legislature with two houses iust as easi- 
Iv as he can a unicameral lawmaking body. Ano- 
ther is that chaos is iust as much nossible in a 
bicameral legislature as in a lawmaking body with 
only one house. 



The President belieyes that a lawmaking body 
like the present AsSomhiy is too easily controlled 
by the nation’s head. This is not ouite accurate. 
If the Assembly is willing to submit to the dicta- 
tion of the President, it is not because there is 
something in the nature of a unicameral law- 
making body that makes this dictation ineyitable. 
Dictation of the chief executiye is possible only 
when the lawmakers are willing to submit to that 
dictation, and this dictation will follow w^hether 
the lawmaking body is unicameral or bicameral. 
Proof of this is the fact that the defunct, bicamer- 
al legislature was as much under the control of 
Mr. Quezon as is the present Assembly. The word 
c 07 fMsL=dQes»not necessarily cari-y eyil odor. Fre- 
quently when a maiority of lawmakers sees eye to 
eve with a chief executive and does not obstruct 
the latter’s program (because it agrees with the 
program)-, the smooth team work between the ex- 
ecutive and legislative departments is intern reted 
as sign of submission of the lawmakers to the dic- 
tation of the chief executive. It can be dictation, 
but only when the lawmakers are willing to be 
dictated to. 



Congress, composed of two houses, was label- 
rubber stamp during the first administra- 
tion of President F D. Roosevelt because Congress 
sunportpd every importarit administration mea- 
sure. But if Congress then was no more than 
President Roosevelt’s rubber stamp, it was be- 
cause Congress was willing to serve in that capa- 
city. When it decided to serve as such no longer. 
Congress showed it could do it. It refused to pass 
Mr. I^o?evelt’s supreme court revamp bill and 
the President had to content himself with a com- 
promise. His administration sugar bill Congress 
siso manhandled. 



What is needed, obviously, if a submissive law- 
making body is to be avoided, is more independ- 
ent-minded lawmakers. Political science has not 



yet invented a legislature impervious to dictation 
And it never will be able to as long as lawma^ 
bodies are open to lawmakers, withput back 

With respect to the chaos being 
in a unicameral legislature than in bbe bicame t 
kind, evidence shows that chaos, resulting 
the legislature backing the chief executive or the 
other way around, can happen in a two-house le- 
gislature as easily as in the one-house kind. 
gine a chief executive belonging to one party and 
a legislature, bicameral or unicameral, over- 
whelmingly controlled by another party, and you 
have all the ingredients necessary for chaos. 
President Wilson broke his health fighting a Re- 
publican Congress. The Philippines had the 
devil of a time when the Philippine legislature 
under the leadersbip of Senate President Quezon 
came to grips’ with the then Governor-General 
Wood. Con cress was bicanieral under Wilson and 
still is. The defunct legislature which fousht 
Wood was bicameral. The bicameral system evid- 
ently has nothing in itself which can prevent 
chaotic strugale between the legislative and ex- 
ecutive departments of a government, if condi- 
tions for conflict are just right. 



The bicameral system may be better than the 
unicameral but better reasons than have been ad« 
vanced by the President must be brought forward 
to prove it. 



FILIPINIZING FILIPINOS 



F ILIPINIZE the F’ilipinos, President Que- 
zon told University of the Philippines 
educators. We like to believe that the Presi- 
dent has in inind the necessity of ending the 
distressing confusion in the mind of the ave- 
rage- product and population of our schools. , 
The students as well as the graduates are 
terribly confused with respect to values af- 
fecting their own lives as well as human re- 
lations. Their books tell them . something j 
actualities contradict it. The impact of 
Western jDrogress has given them an unmis- 
takable Occidental outlook without complete- 
ly freeing them from the hold of national . 
traditions. They have come to, want many 
things without the means with which to 
realize them. 






It is all very well to say to them, “Get 
wTiat is worthwhile of the Occident and re- 
tain what is good and desirable of your na- 
tional traits and traditions,” but what is 
worthwhile of the Occident and good and de- 
sirable of local traits and traditions? Herein 
lies the confusion. And herein lies the prob- 
lem of our educators.

Quezon Raps Youth's 

Meddling- In Politics 



^'-yurV' ^ 



Youth in politics was placed on 
the carpet by President Quezon 
in his weekly conference last 
Wednesday. He criticized the 
recent political movements 
among local students and the 
youths^ apparent desire to try to 
decide major government policies 
He believes that the young jjeo- 
ple are neither fully trained nor 
sufficiently experienced to dic- 
tate the policies of the govern- 
ment. Let the grey political 
heads, who are less prone to be 
moved by passion, and more 
amenable to reasoiu do such 
i burdensome tasks, the President 
said in effect. 

The President '^advises the 
youth to confine its interest in 
public affairs to college debating 
clubs, instead of dabbling in ac- 
tive politics. 

In decrying this dangerous 
tendency of our young people, he 
cited the examples of Spain, Jap- 
an, and China where the students 
tried to decide the major policies 
of the government and in one of 
these countries they went to the 
extent of assassinating their 
prime minister. “Do you want 
that to happen here?” asked the 
President. \. 

In passing, the chief executive 
ilso rapped .the fri\ olous and ex- 
travagant tendencies of the pre- 
sent age, blamed the press for 
promoting beauty and popularity 
contest which tend to encourage 
the spirit of frivolity. 

Commenting on the editorial of 
a local daily newspaper last Sun- 
day, President Quezon declared 
that, when, in his message to the 



Assembly, he mentioned that the 
children of the rich are more 
prone to give more interest to 
frivolities rather than to soyious 
^york, he said that the rich" can 
live in luxury because they have 
the means, but he did not ^hy 
that they do so. He declared it 
v^as not a criticism against th^ 
lich but rather a plea tliat more 
people should live as the middle 
class do. 

In answer to' that part of the 
editoz'ial which blames the high- 
er authorities for the frivolities, 
the President said that as head 
of the government, he has to ar- 
range for a certain amount «of so- 
cial life, this being done by the 
head? of . all nations. 

Observed he; “You must 'un- 
derstand that these are facts of 
life. I am not attempting to 
create a society of monks, and, 
if I did, I would fail. There is 
a difference between those who 
have earned their wealth crijoy- 
ing -it, and those who are just 
preparing to make money.’*

PI DAILY WAGE 1

The prosperity o.f.the provincial 
government which is shown in the 
heavy collection of taxes, is to be 
sared with propertionately by the 
loberers in the public works by vir- 
tue of a resolution approved by 
the provincial board. 

In accordance with the Qu^on 
policy of social justice and of the 
Executive Order defining the mini- 
mum wage to the loborers working 
in the public works, Board Member 
Genaro Maramara presented a re- 
solution providing^ the minimum 
daily wage of all laborers of the 
provincial government of Cebu from 
P.75 to PI. 00 when working with- 
in the City of - Cebu, and from 
P.60 to P. 80. when working in the 



[ /ther parts oFthe province of 
Cebu outside tbe City. This new 
scale of the laborers’ wages was 
effective since the first day of this 
month. 

The provincial government nas 
shown a r^markah’e progress with- 
in the last few months judging 
from the collection of taxes. The 
author of the resolution which 
gives the minimum wage to the la- 
borers explained yesterday that the 
provincial hoard was . merely acting 
in line with the Quezon policy of 
social justice. As a matter of -fact, 
,he said, it has been, the intention 
of the provincial board to help 
the laboring c’ass working under 
the provincial government to give- 
them a living wage as much as tne ■ 
provincial funds can afforo. 

A’l chiefs of the different de- 
partments o the, 'provincial govern- 
'ment have been notified to can. 
out the provisions of this resolu- 
'tion with regard to laborers under 
their -respective departments.^

ALAS EXPLAINS 



Budget Provision Covering Na- 
vigation Division 



Secrctaiy of Finanq<»-‘'Aiitomo 
dc las Alas has written to Pres- 
ident Manuel L. Quezon informing 
the PresT3^nt of the absence of a 
provision in the budget for 1938 
restoring the original salaries of 
the personnel of the navigation 
division of the bureau of customs, 
including the officers of customs 
coastguards. 

The secretary of finance ex- 
plained that the failure to insert 
the corresponding provision was 
not due to a deliberate act. When 
he called the bureau chiefs to a 
conference, they were given in- 
struction to prepare a budget so 
fJiat the salaries of the officials 
and employees w’ould be divisible 
bv five. William Baxter, chief of 
the division of hulls and boilers 
of the bureau of customs, who 
was to prcp*arc the budget, foi 
the navigation division, was pic- 
sent at that conference. ; 

However, the cabinet decided to 
effect a restoration of the salaries 
of officials and employees, ex- 
cept in the case of those who 
have had substantial increase dur- 
ing the year. Secretaiy Alas is- 
sued further instructions to carry 
out the decision of the cabinet in 
the preparation of the 1938 bud- 
get. When this instimctlon was 
given out it was explained; Box- 
ter was ill with typhoid fever and 
did not receive the instimction. 
When he recovered, he prepared 
his division’s budget in accord- 
ance with the first instimctipm 
and this budget was included in 
the general budgetary plan, which 
1 'vvas approved by the cabinet an 
submitted by the Pi-csident to 
the National Assembly. It was 
only after this that the oversight 
■was discovered.

discussion of a ix>ssible dominion 
status for the Philippines.

nothing doing,” the editorial assert- 
ed. “The Filipinos, or their leaders, 
hate us wdien things are ouict in tiic 
Fai* East and warm up to us in 
hea\y \veaUicr. 

“They asked for freedom and 
Congress granted it. They ought 
now to be made to take \vhat they 
asked for.”

What good is social justice in a country where there is no 
law and order? Of what benefit are minimum wage scales 
md eight-hour labor laws and barrio roads and artesian wells 
if the average citizen has no security either in person or in 
property? Can a man whose home is likely to be broken into 
md robbed at any time of the day and night, whose skull is 
ikely to be cracked if he ventures out into a street after daik 
ian such a man get the benefits of any social justice program, 
10 matter how carefully planned or how skilfully executed? 

The facts are plain. Manila, capital city of the Philip- 
pines, Pearl of the Orient Seas, is so badly policed that the 
jriminal elements are running wild. Everyone admits present 
londitions are deplorable. Last week, for instance, the Herald 
said that -Confidence in the ability of the city government to 

Maintain peace, order and tranquillity is as low 
in the stock market.” The Tribune pleaded with city otticiais

pozos artesianos, si el hombre de la calle no goza de seguridad 
personal ni en lo que atane a su propiedad? ^Acaso un hombre 
cuya casa esta expuesta a ser asaltada y robada a cualquier hora 
del dia y de la-noche, y cuya vida esta pendiente de un hilo si 
se lanza a la calle al anochecer, puede beneficiarse de un nro 
grama de justicia social, por muy bien trazado y llevado a cahn 
que este dicho programa? 

Los hechos son claros. Manila, la capital de FiliniTiac! la 
Perla del Mar de Oriente, esta tan abandonada por la nnhVfa

Quezon Hara Un Largo

De Su Restablecimiento 

(Especial pave “El Sol”) 

Manilla,. Nov. 26. — Ej pre- 



SWBFTEB PACE, BATHEJB— One observer of the 
local political scene makes the remark that the prepa- 
rations for nation building have today slowed down 
somewhat from the pace set two years ago, suggesting 
as reason therefor the fading away of the glamour 
with which the ushering in of a new nation is usually 
attended. * 



I consider the cause assigned as flimsy as the sup- 
liosed result is untrue. Of the pace of the past twelve- 
month of the Commonwealth, my impression h^, on 
the contrary, been one of a swift and rather bewilder- 
ing succession of events, of policies laid down, of goals 
achieved or about to be achieved. 

In any estimate of the extent to which the Com- 
monwealth government has brought the country nearer 
the goal of political freedom and economic security, it 
is necessary to take into account not only what things 
have been done but what things have happened. 

At no other time has the economic program of the 
administration been more strongly emphasized than in 
the past few months. Considered purely as a talking 
point, social justice as a goal of the government has re- 
ceived more attention than at any other time, owing 
principally to President Quezon’s attack against the ju- 
diciary, his speech in Iloilo, and his message to the Na- 
tional Assembly. 

The accomplishments of the past year have been 
accurately itemized for the curious and the skeptical to 
examine. To me, however, it is npt these various items 



considered either singly or iii mass, nor even the full 
impact of their total 'significance that matters. Rather 
am I impressed, inspired even, by the better prospects 
to which present events inevitably lead the view. 

What the second anniversary of the Commonwealth 
means, as far as I can see, is that the preliminary steps 
in nation building have already been taken. The ground 
has been laid with care. Barring unforeseen events, es- 
pecially those of an international character over which 
we shall have little or no control, it is inevitable that 
from now on the building up of the superstructure will 
be undertaken at an accelerated pace. 

The revision of oun taxation system, the liquidation 
of our present agrarian relations, among the most ur- 
gent of our domestic problems, are even now receiving 
first consideration by the state. President Quezon has a 
profound personal interest in these twin issues, and is 
certain to throw the full weight of his influence 
der that they may bd resolved speedily and yet also with.

A DRAMATIC CHANGE 



There probabj}- v^'ili be no lasting un- 
favoi'able effect of the circumstances under 
which Presid ent QuezL Cm-^^rT^e very last 
minute declined to lay the cornerstone of the 
Boy Scout building, i'tn fact the dramatic 
; incident possibly may have a publicity value 
sufficient to make it an asset in the long 
run. But, regardless of whether it was his 
fault or the fault of somebody else, the Pres- 
ident should haye known earlier that the 
general plans as to location and building 
were such that he would to balk the 

whole program. 

There hardly can be any quesiion from 
any source about the soundness of the Pres- 
ident’s ob.iection— personal and official — to 
the location. It is true too that it was better 
to call a dramatic halt at the last minute than 
' to go on through with something that wa.s 
fundamentally incorrect. But this is one of 
the cases in which wm fittingly can repeat, 
^‘Better late than never, 'but better never 



late.”

tizing of President Quezon’s pledge of per- 
sonal and official support— generous supp^ 
it is too — ^when the proper location is fmi 
and plans are remade to 4o a better .lob oi 
meeting the needs of the SVO^vlng orga.rnz - 
i-nin However, the basic merits of the case 
a«d' tvS worthiness of the cause stand un- 



changed from what they would have be<^ if 
the planning had been such as to avoid this 
sudden scrambling of the program. ; 

We like to think that the government’s i 
.support is a matter of fundamental n'ght and 
in no sense a way out of an embarrassing 
situation created by the necessitj^ of an 
abrupt and dramatic changing of . the pro- 
gram and the plans. In other words the 
government should not have to be considered 
as in the position of having to buy the boy.s 
off the Intramuros wall even though that 
deal might -work to the scouts’- immediate

PRESERVE THE WALLS 

While asserting his belief in the value of the Boy Scout 
inovement, and piacing* himself on record as being only too wil- 
ling to help it along, President Quezon yesterday showed a 
marked sense of fifness of things when he refused to permit the 
construction of the proposed headquarters of the organiza- 
tion on the site selected for it, atop the ancient battiements 
which give the Walled City its name. 

Manila is history in land and stone. It is the uttermost 
limit reached by the wave of European civilization which started 
from Atlantic coast of Europe four centuries and more ago. 

It is the farthest point at which that wave deposited seeds that 
took root and flourished. Not a single physcial sign of that 
splendid growth should be mutilated, marred, or even altered. 

To build a modern edifice on the Manila city wall, however 
worthy the purpose of the structure may be, would be as des- 
tructive, in the historical and esthetic senses, as to establish a 
trade school in the Acropolis at Athens. 

— The Tribune.

Improvement And Maintenance Will Be 
Undertaken By Central Government 



I^esident Quezon last night is- 
sued'^ a* ox^r designating five 
coastal cities and 56 towns and 
municip^ districts as national 
pcrts. In addition to the 9 regu- 
lar ports of call, Manila, Cebu, 
Iloilo, Zainbbanga, Davao, Jolo, 
liegaspi, Mainbulao, and Aparri, 
the President named three others, 
Pului>andan, Hondagua and San 
lldefonso. 

According to the order, the im- 
provement and maintenance of 
national ports shall be undertak- 
en and financed by the national 
government, and their administra- 
tion and opei*ation shall be under 
the direct supervision and con- 
trol of the insular collector of 
customs. The municipal ports 
shall be maintained by the muni- 
cipalities . 

Besides the new national ports, 
the President may hereafter de- 
clare national ports other munici- 
pal- ports when it is considered 
necessary to do so in the interest 
of commerce, and for enforcement 
of customs regulations, or for na- 
tional defense purposes. 

The municipal ports declared by 
the President are as follows: 

Agusan, (1); Nasipit; Albay, (2) 
li'Bgaspi (port of entry); Albay, (3) 
Tabaco;.= Albay (4) Virac (Cantan- 
duanes); Antique (5) San Jose de 
Buenavista; Bataan, (6) Marive- 
les; Batanes, (7) Basco; Batanes, 
(S) Contra Costa; Batangas, (9) 
Batangas; Batangas, (10) Nasug- 
bu; Bohol, (11) Tagbilaran; Ca- 
gayan, (12) Aparri; (port of en- 
try) ; ^amarines Norte, (13) Jose 
3 ?angariiban (Mambulao) (port of 



.entry); Oapiz, (14) Capiz; Capiz, 
(15) New Washington; Cebu, (16) 
Cebu (port of entry); Cebu, (17) 
Toledo.
